codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth VP9 (180P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 180P" data-transcodekey="180p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" data-height="180" data-bandwidth="219416" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/bd/A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv/A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="267792" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/bd/A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv/A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="318760" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/bd/A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv/A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv.360p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="411592" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/bd/A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv/A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="575256" data-framerate="25"/&gt;&lt;track src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?title=TimedText:A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv.ko.srt&amp;amp;action=raw&amp;amp;ctype=text%2Fx-srt" kind="subtitles" type="text/x-srt" srclang="ko" label="&#54620;&#44397;&#50612; (ko) subtitles" data-mwtitle="TimedText:A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv.ko.srt" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-dir="ltr"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:A Swift Tour of M31.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv/220px--A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Swift_Tour_of_M31.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Neil_Gehrels_Swift_Observatory" title="Neil Gehrels Swift Observatory">Swift</a> Tour of Andromeda Galaxy.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andromeda_galaxy_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4500" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Andromeda_galaxy_2.jpg/220px-Andromeda_galaxy_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Andromeda_galaxy_2.jpg/330px-Andromeda_galaxy_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Andromeda_galaxy_2.jpg/440px-Andromeda_galaxy_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Andromeda_galaxy_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/GALEX" title="GALEX">Galaxy Evolution Explorer</a> image of the Andromeda Galaxy. The bands of blue-white making up the galaxy\'s striking rings are neighborhoods that harbor hot, young, massive stars. Dark blue-grey lanes of cooler dust show up starkly against these bright rings, tracing the regions where star formation is currently taking place in dense cloudy cocoons. When observed in visible light, Andromeda Galaxy\'s rings look more like spiral arms. The ultraviolet view shows that these arms more closely resemble the ring-like structure previously observed in infrared wavelengths with NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer Space Telescope</a>. Astronomers using the latter interpreted these rings as evidence that the galaxy was involved in a direct collision with its neighbor, M32, more than 200 million years ago.</div></div></div>\n<p>Based on its appearance in visible light, the Andromeda Galaxy is classified as an SA(s)b galaxy in the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_morphological_classification#De_Vaucouleurs_system" title="Galaxy morphological classification">de Vaucouleurs&#8211;Sandage extended classification system</a> of spiral galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ned_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-ned-1">[1]</a></sup> However, data from the <a href="/wiki/2MASS" title="2MASS">2MASS</a> survey showed that Andromeda is actually a <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">barred spiral galaxy</a>, like the Milky Way, with Andromeda\'s bar oriented along its long axis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beaton_2006_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beaton_2006-63">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2005, astronomers used the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keck_telescopes" title="Keck telescopes">Keck telescopes</a> to show that the tenuous sprinkle of stars extending outward from the galaxy is actually part of the main disk itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman_et_al_2006_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman_et_al_2006-13">[11]</a></sup> This means that the spiral disk of stars in the Andromeda Galaxy is three times larger in diameter than previously estimated. This constitutes evidence that there is a vast, extended stellar disk that makes the galaxy more than 220,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> (67,000 <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a>) in diameter. Previously, estimates of the Andromeda Galaxy\'s size ranged from 70,000 to 120,000 light-years (21,000 to 37,000&#160;pc) across.\n</p><p>The galaxy is inclined an estimated 77&#176; relative to Earth (where an angle of 90&#176; would be viewed directly from the side). Analysis of the cross-sectional shape of the galaxy appears to demonstrate a pronounced, S-shaped warp, rather than just a flat disk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UC_Santa_Cruz_2001_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-UC_Santa_Cruz_2001-64">[60]</a></sup> A possible cause of such a warp could be gravitational interaction with the satellite galaxies near the Andromeda Galaxy. The Galaxy <a href="/wiki/Triangulum_Galaxy" title="Triangulum Galaxy">M33</a> could be responsible for some warp in Andromeda\'s arms, though more precise distances and radial velocities are required.\n</p><p>Spectroscopic studies have provided detailed measurements of the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_rotation_curve" title="Galaxy rotation curve">rotational velocity of the Andromeda Galaxy</a> as a function of radial distance from the core. The rotational velocity has a maximum value of 225 kilometres per second (140&#160;mi/s) at 1,300 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> (82,000,000 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a>) from the core, and it has its minimum possibly as low as 50 kilometres per second (31&#160;mi/s) at 7,000 light-years (440,000,000&#160;AU) from the core. Further out, rotational velocity rises out to a radius of 33,000 light-years (2.1<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;AU), where it reaches a peak of 250 kilometres per second (160&#160;mi/s). The velocities slowly decline beyond that distance, dropping to around 200 kilometres per second (120&#160;mi/s) at 80,000 light-years (5.1<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;AU). These velocity measurements imply a concentrated mass of about 6<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">9</span></sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub></a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxy_nucleus" title="Galaxy nucleus">nucleus</a>. The total mass of the galaxy increases <a href="/wiki/Linearity" title="Linearity">linearly</a> out to 45,000 light-years (2.8<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;AU), then more slowly beyond that radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin_&amp;_Ford_1970_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin_&amp;_Ford_1970-65">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy#Spiral_arms" title="Spiral galaxy">spiral arms</a> of the Andromeda Galaxy are outlined by a series of <a href="/wiki/H_II_region" title="H II region">HII regions</a>, first studied in great detail by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Baade" title="Walter Baade">Walter Baade</a> and described by him as resembling "beads on a string". His studies show two spiral arms that appear to be tightly wound, although they are more widely spaced than in our galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arp_1964_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arp_1964-66">[62]</a></sup> His descriptions of the spiral structure, as each arm crosses the major axis of the Andromeda Galaxy, are as follows<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergh1991_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bergh1991-67">[63]</a></sup><sup>&#167;pp1062</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hodge1966_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hodge1966-68">[64]</a></sup><sup>&#167;pp92</sup>:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Baade\'s spiral arms of M31\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Arms (N=cross M31\'s major axis at north, S=cross M31\'s major axis at south)\n</th>\n<th>Distance from center (<a href="/wiki/Minute_and_second_of_arc" title="Minute and second of arc">arcminutes</a>) (N*/S*)\n</th>\n<th>Distance from center (kpc) (N*/S*)\n</th>\n<th>Notes\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">N1/S1</td>\n<td>3.4/1.7</td>\n<td>0.7/0.4</td>\n<td>Dust arms with no <a href="/wiki/Stellar_kinematics#OB_associations" title="Stellar kinematics">OB associations</a> of <a href="/wiki/H_II_region" title="H II region">HII regions</a>.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">N2/S2</td>\n<td>8.0/10.0</td>\n<td>1.7/2.1</td>\n<td>Dust arms with some OB associations.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">N3/S3</td>\n<td>25/30</td>\n<td>5.3/6.3</td>\n<td>As per N2/S2, but with some HII regions too.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">N4/S4</td>\n<td>50/47</td>\n<td>11/9.9</td>\n<td>Large numbers of OB associations, HII regions, and little dust.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">N5/S5</td>\n<td>70/66</td>\n<td>15/14</td>\n<td>As per N4/S4 but much fainter.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">N6/S6</td>\n<td>91/95</td>\n<td>19/20</td>\n<td>Loose OB associations. No dust visible.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">N7/S7</td>\n<td>110/116</td>\n<td>23/24</td>\n<td>As per N6/S6 but fainter and inconspicuous.\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Since the Andromeda Galaxy is seen close to edge-on, it is difficult to study its spiral structure. Rectified images of the galaxy seem to show a fairly normal spiral galaxy, exhibiting two continuous trailing arms that are separated from each other by a minimum of about 13,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> (820,000,000 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a>) and that can be followed outward from a distance of roughly 1,600 light-years (100,000,000&#160;AU) from the core. Alternative spiral structures have been proposed such as a single spiral arm<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simien1978_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simien1978-69">[65]</a></sup> or a <a href="/wiki/Flocculent_spiral_galaxy" title="Flocculent spiral galaxy">flocculent</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haas2000_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haas2000-70">[66]</a></sup> pattern of long, filamentary, and thick spiral arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ned_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-ned-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walterbos1988_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walterbos1988-71">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most likely cause of the distortions of the spiral pattern is thought to be interaction with galaxy satellites <a href="/wiki/Messier_32" title="Messier 32">M32</a> and <a href="/wiki/Messier_110" title="Messier 110">M110</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon2006_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon2006-72">[68]</a></sup> This can be seen by the displacement of the <a href="/wiki/H_I_region" title="H I region">neutral hydrogen clouds</a> from the stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Braun_1991_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Braun_1991-73">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1998, images from the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Infrared_Space_Observatory" title="Infrared Space Observatory">Infrared Space Observatory</a> demonstrated that the overall form of the Andromeda Galaxy may be transitioning into a <a href="/wiki/Ring_galaxy" title="Ring galaxy">ring galaxy</a>. The gas and dust within the galaxy is generally formed into several overlapping rings, with a particularly prominent ring formed at a radius of 32,000 light-years (2.0<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;AU) (10 kiloparsecs) from the core,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ESA_1998-10-14_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-ESA_1998-10-14-74">[70]</a></sup> nicknamed by some astronomers the <i>ring of fire</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-M31youngclusters_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-M31youngclusters-75">[71]</a></sup> This ring is hidden from visible light images of the galaxy because it is composed primarily of cold dust, and most of the star formation that is taking place in the Andromeda Galaxy is concentrated there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andromedan10kpcring_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andromedan10kpcring-76">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Later studies with the help of the <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer Space Telescope</a> showed how Andromeda Galaxy\'s spiral structure in the infrared appears to be composed of two spiral arms that emerge from a central bar and continue beyond the large ring mentioned above. Those arms, however, are not continuous and have a segmented structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon2006_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon2006-72">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Close examination of the inner region of the Andromeda Galaxy with the same telescope also showed a smaller dust ring that is believed to have been caused by the interaction with M32 more than 200&#160; million years ago. Simulations show that the smaller galaxy passed through the disk of the Andromeda Galaxy along the latter\'s polar axis. This collision stripped more than half the mass from the smaller M32 and created the ring structures in Andromeda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvard-Smithsonian_2006-10-18_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harvard-Smithsonian_2006-10-18-77">[73]</a></sup>\nIt is the co-existence of the long-known large ring-like feature in the gas of Messier 31, together with this newly discovered inner ring-like structure, offset from the barycenter, that suggested a nearly head-on collision with the satellite M32, a milder version of the <a href="/wiki/Cartwheel_Galaxy" title="Cartwheel Galaxy">Cartwheel encounter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Block2006_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Block2006-78">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Studies of the extended halo of the Andromeda Galaxy show that it is roughly comparable to that of the Milky Way, with stars in the halo being generally "<a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metal-poor</a>", and increasingly so with greater distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalirai_et_al_2006_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kalirai_et_al_2006-46">[43]</a></sup> This evidence indicates that the two galaxies have followed similar evolutionary paths. They are likely to have accreted and assimilated about 100&#8211;200 low-mass galaxies during the past 12 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bullock_&amp;_Johnston_2005_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bullock_&amp;_Johnston_2005-79">[75]</a></sup> The stars in the extended halos of the Andromeda Galaxy and the Milky Way may extend nearly one third the distance separating the two galaxies.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nucleus">Nucleus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andromeda_Galaxy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nucleus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Double_Nucleus_of_the_Andromeda_Galaxy_(M31).tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2318" data-file-width="2881" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Double_Nucleus_of_the_Andromeda_Galaxy_%28M31%29.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Double_Nucleus_of_the_Andromeda_Galaxy_%28M31%29.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Double_Nucleus_of_the_Andromeda_Galaxy_%28M31%29.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Double_Nucleus_of_the_Andromeda_Galaxy_%28M31%29.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Double_Nucleus_of_the_Andromeda_Galaxy_%28M31%29.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Double_Nucleus_of_the_Andromeda_Galaxy_%28M31%29.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Double_Nucleus_of_the_Andromeda_Galaxy_(M31).tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hubble image of the Andromeda Galaxy core showing possible double structure. <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>/<a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA</a>&#160;photo.</div></div></div>\n<p>M31 is known to harbor a dense and compact star cluster at its very center. In a large telescope it creates a visual impression of a star embedded in the more diffuse surrounding bulge. In 1991, the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> was used to image Andromeda Galaxy\'s inner nucleus. The nucleus consists of two concentrations separated by 1.5 <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a> (4.9&#160;<a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">ly</a>). The brighter concentration, designated as P1, is offset from the center of the galaxy. The dimmer concentration, P2, falls at the true center of the galaxy and contains a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a> measured at 3&#8211;5 &#215; 10<sup>7</sup> <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub></a> in 1993,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lauer_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lauer-80">[76]</a></sup> and at 1.1&#8211;2.3 &#215; 10<sup>8</sup> <var>M</var><sub>&#9737;</sub> in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bender_et_al_2005_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bender_et_al_2005-81">[77]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Velocity_dispersion" title="Velocity dispersion">velocity dispersion</a> of material around it is measured to be &#8776; 160&#160;km/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gebhardt_et_al_2000_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gebhardt_et_al_2000-82">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M31_Core_in_X-rays.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="431" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/M31_Core_in_X-rays.jpg/220px-M31_Core_in_X-rays.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/M31_Core_in_X-rays.jpg/330px-M31_Core_in_X-rays.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/M31_Core_in_X-rays.jpg/440px-M31_Core_in_X-rays.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M31_Core_in_X-rays.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-ray Observatory">Chandra X-ray telescope</a> image of the center of Andromeda Galaxy. A number of X-ray sources, likely X-ray binary stars, within the galaxy\'s central region appear as yellowish dots. The blue source at the center is at the position of the <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black hole</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>It has been proposed that the observed double nucleus could be explained if P1 is the projection of a disk of stars in an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric orbit</a> around the central black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tremaine_1995_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tremaine_1995-83">[79]</a></sup> The eccentricity is such that stars linger at the orbital <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">apocenter</a>, creating a concentration of stars. P2 also contains a compact disk of hot, <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">spectral-class</a> A stars. The A stars are not evident in redder filters, but in blue and ultraviolet light they dominate the nucleus, causing P2 to appear more prominent than P1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubblesite_1993-07-20_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-hubblesite_1993-07-20-84">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While at the initial time of its discovery it was hypothesized that the brighter portion of the double nucleus is the remnant of a small galaxy "cannibalized" by Andromeda Galaxy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schewe_&amp;_Stein_1993_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schewe_&amp;_Stein_1993-85">[81]</a></sup> this is no longer considered a viable explanation, largely because such a nucleus would have an exceedingly short lifetime due to <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal disruption</a> by the central black hole. While this could be partially resolved if P1 had its own black hole to stabilize it, the distribution of stars in P1 does not suggest that there is a black hole at its center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tremaine_1995_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tremaine_1995-83">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discrete_sources">Discrete sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andromeda_Galaxy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Discrete sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA20061_-_Andromeda_in_High-Energy_X-rays,_Figure_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3538" data-file-width="6289" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/PIA20061_-_Andromeda_in_High-Energy_X-rays%2C_Figure_1.jpg/400px-PIA20061_-_Andromeda_in_High-Energy_X-rays%2C_Figure_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/PIA20061_-_Andromeda_in_High-Energy_X-rays%2C_Figure_1.jpg/600px-PIA20061_-_Andromeda_in_High-Energy_X-rays%2C_Figure_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/PIA20061_-_Andromeda_in_High-Energy_X-rays%2C_Figure_1.jpg/800px-PIA20061_-_Andromeda_in_High-Energy_X-rays%2C_Figure_1.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA20061_-_Andromeda_in_High-Energy_X-rays,_Figure_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Andromeda Galaxy in high-energy X-ray and ultraviolet light (released 5 January 2016).</div></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andromeda_active_core.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3750" data-file-width="5000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Andromeda_active_core.jpg/220px-Andromeda_active_core.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Andromeda_active_core.jpg/330px-Andromeda_active_core.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Andromeda_active_core.jpg/440px-Andromeda_active_core.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Andromeda_active_core.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s concept of the Andromeda Galaxy core showing a view across a disk of young, blue stars encircling a supermassive black hole. <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>/<a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA</a>&#160;photo.</div></div></div>\n<p>Apparently, by late 1968, no X-rays had been detected from the Andromeda Galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fujimoto_et_al_1969_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fujimoto_et_al_1969-86">[82]</a></sup> A balloon flight on October 20, 1970, set an upper limit for detectable hard X-rays from the Andromeda Galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_1973_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_1973-87">[83]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Swift" title="Swift">Swift</a> BAT all-sky survey successfully detected hard X-rays coming from a region centered 6 arcseconds away from the galaxy center. The emission above 25 keV was later found to be originating from a single source named <a href="/wiki/3XMM_J004232.1%2B411314" title="3XMM J004232.1+411314">3XMM J004232.1+411314</a>, and identified as a binary system where a compact object (a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a>) accretes matter from a star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marelli_et_al._2017_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marelli_et_al._2017-88">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Multiple X-ray sources have since been detected in the Andromeda Galaxy, using observations from the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>\'s (ESA) <a href="/wiki/XMM-Newton" title="XMM-Newton">XMM-Newton</a> orbiting observatory. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Robin_Barnard&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Robin Barnard (page does not exist)">Robin Barnard</a> <i>et al.</i> hypothesized that these are candidate black holes or <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>, which are heating the incoming gas to millions of kelvins and emitting X-rays. Neutron stars and black holes can be distinguished mainly by measuring their masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnard_Kolb_&amp;_Osborne_2005_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnard_Kolb_&amp;_Osborne_2005-89">[85]</a></sup> An observation campaign of <a href="/wiki/NuSTAR" title="NuSTAR">NuSTAR</a> space mission identified 40 objects of this kind in the galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are approximately 460 <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">globular clusters</a> associated with the Andromeda Galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barmby_&amp;_Huchra_2001_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barmby_&amp;_Huchra_2001-91">[87]</a></sup> The most massive of these clusters, identified as <a href="/wiki/Mayall_II" title="Mayall II">Mayall II</a>, nicknamed Globular One, has a greater luminosity than any other known globular cluster in the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a> of galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubblesite_1996-04-24_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-hubblesite_1996-04-24-92">[88]</a></sup> It contains several million stars, and is about twice as luminous as <a href="/wiki/Omega_Centauri" title="Omega Centauri">Omega Centauri</a>, the brightest known globular cluster in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>. Globular One (or G1) has several stellar populations and a structure too massive for an ordinary globular. As a result, some consider G1 to be the remnant core of a <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy" title="Dwarf galaxy">dwarf galaxy</a> that was consumed by Andromeda in the distant past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meylan_et_al_2001_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meylan_et_al_2001-93">[89]</a></sup> The globular with the greatest apparent brightness is <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=G76&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="G76 (page does not exist)">G76</a> which is located in the south-west arm\'s eastern half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSOG_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSOG-21">[19]</a></sup>\nAnother massive globular cluster, named 037-B327 and discovered in 2006 as is heavily reddened by the Andromeda Galaxy\'s <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">interstellar dust</a>, was thought to be more massive than G1 and the largest cluster of the Local Group;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ma06_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ma06-94">[90]</a></sup> however, other studies have shown it is actually similar in properties to G1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen06_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen06-95">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Star_cluster_in_the_Andromeda_galaxy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Star_cluster_in_the_Andromeda_galaxy.jpg/220px-Star_cluster_in_the_Andromeda_galaxy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Star_cluster_in_the_Andromeda_galaxy.jpg/330px-Star_cluster_in_the_Andromeda_galaxy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Star_cluster_in_the_Andromeda_galaxy.jpg/440px-Star_cluster_in_the_Andromeda_galaxy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Star_cluster_in_the_Andromeda_galaxy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Star clusters in the Andromeda Galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Unlike the globular clusters of the Milky Way, which show a relatively low age dispersion, Andromeda Galaxy\'s globular clusters have a much larger range of ages: from systems as old as the galaxy itself to much younger systems, with ages between a few hundred million years to five billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bu06_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bu06-97">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2005, astronomers discovered a completely new type of star cluster in the Andromeda Galaxy. The new-found clusters contain hundreds of thousands of stars, a similar number of stars that can be found in globular clusters. What distinguishes them from the globular clusters is that they are much larger&#8212;several hundred light-years across&#8212;and hundreds of times less dense. The distances between the stars are, therefore, much greater within the newly discovered extended clusters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huxor_et_al_2005_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huxor_et_al_2005-98">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2012, a <a href="/wiki/Microquasar" title="Microquasar">microquasar</a>, a radio burst emanating from a smaller black hole, was detected in the Andromeda Galaxy. The progenitor black hole is located near the galactic center and has about 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_masses" title="Solar masses"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\begin{smallmatrix}M_{\\odot }\\end{smallmatrix}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle scriptlevel="1">\n<mtable columnspacing="0.333em" displaystyle="false" rowspacing=".2em">\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<msub>\n<mi>M</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>&#8857;<!-- &#8857; --></mo>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n</mtable>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\begin{smallmatrix}M_{\\odot }\\end{smallmatrix}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\begin{smallmatrix}M_{\\odot }\\end{smallmatrix}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4215f605f7e260ab23fb5012a2aed3a31172740c" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:3.548ex; height:1.843ex;"/></span></a>. Discovered through a data collected by the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/XMM-Newton" title="XMM-Newton">XMM-Newton</a> probe, and subsequently observed by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Neil_Gehrels_Swift_Observatory" title="Neil Gehrels Swift Observatory">Swift Gamma-Ray Burst Mission</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chandra_X-Ray_Observatory" title="Chandra X-Ray Observatory">Chandra X-Ray Observatory</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Array" title="Very Large Array">Very Large Array</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Very_Long_Baseline_Array" title="Very Long Baseline Array">Very Long Baseline Array</a>, the microquasar was the first observed within the Andromeda Galaxy and the first outside of the Milky Way Galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prostak-2012_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-prostak-2012-99">[95]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Satellites">Satellites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andromeda_Galaxy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Satellites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andromeda%27s_satellite_galaxies" title="Andromeda\'s satellite galaxies">Andromeda\'s satellite galaxies</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M31_09-01-2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/M31_09-01-2011.jpg/250px-M31_09-01-2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/M31_09-01-2011.jpg/375px-M31_09-01-2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/M31_09-01-2011.jpg/500px-M31_09-01-2011.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M31_09-01-2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Messier 32 is to the left of the center, Messier 110 is to the bottom-right of the center.</div></div></div>\n<p>Like the Milky Way, the Andromeda Galaxy has <a href="/wiki/Satellite_galaxy" title="Satellite galaxy">satellite galaxies</a>, consisting of 14 known <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy" title="Dwarf galaxy">dwarf galaxies</a>. The best known and most readily observed satellite galaxies are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliptical_Galaxy_M32" title="Elliptical Galaxy M32">M32</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliptical_Galaxy_M110" title="Elliptical Galaxy M110">M110</a>. Based on current evidence, it appears that M32 underwent a close encounter with the Andromeda Galaxy in the past. M32 may once have been a larger galaxy that had its stellar disk removed by M31, and underwent a sharp increase of <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">star formation</a> in the core region, which lasted until the relatively recent past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bekki_et_al_2001_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bekki_et_al_2001-100">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>M110 also appears to be interacting with the Andromeda Galaxy, and astronomers have found in the halo of the latter a stream of metal-rich stars that appear to have been stripped from these satellite galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ibata_et_al_2001_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ibata_et_al_2001-101">[97]</a></sup> M110 does contain a dusty lane, which may indicate recent or ongoing star formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_2000_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young_2000-102">[98]</a></sup> M32 have young population as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RWM09_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-RWM09-103">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2006, it was discovered that nine of the satellite galaxies lie in a plane that intersects the core of the Andromeda Galaxy; they are not randomly arranged as would be expected from independent interactions. This may indicate a common tidal origin for the satellites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koch_&amp;_Grebel_2006_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koch_&amp;_Grebel_2006-104">[100]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Collision_with_the_Milky_Way">Collision with the Milky Way</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andromeda_Galaxy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Collision with the Milky Way">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Andromeda%E2%80%93Milky_Way_collision" title="Andromeda&#8211;Milky Way collision">Andromeda&#8211;Milky Way collision</a></div>\n<p>The Andromeda Galaxy is approaching the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> at about 110 kilometres per second (68 miles per second).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature-105">[101]</a></sup> It has been measured approaching relative to our Sun at around 300&#160;km/s (190&#160;mi/s)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ned_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-ned-1">[1]</a></sup> as the Sun orbits around the center of our galaxy at a speed of approximately 225&#160;km/s (140&#160;mi/s). This makes the Andromeda Galaxy one of about 100 observable <a href="/wiki/Blueshift" title="Blueshift">blueshifted</a> galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup> Andromeda Galaxy\'s tangential or sideways velocity with respect to the Milky Way is relatively much smaller than the approaching velocity and therefore it is expected to collide directly with the Milky Way in about 4&#160;billion years. A likely outcome of the collision is that the <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_merger" title="Galaxy merger">galaxies will merge</a> to form a giant <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_galaxy" title="Elliptical galaxy">elliptical galaxy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cox_&amp;_Loeb_2008_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cox_&amp;_Loeb_2008-107">[103]</a></sup> or perhaps even a large <a href="/wiki/Disc_galaxy" title="Disc galaxy">disc galaxy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ueda2014_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ueda2014-17">[15]</a></sup> Such events are frequent among the galaxies in <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_group" title="Galaxy group">galaxy groups</a>. The fate of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> in the event of a collision is currently unknown. Before the galaxies merge, there is a small chance that the Solar System could be ejected from the Milky Way or join the Andromeda Galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain_2007_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cain_2007-108">[104]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Amateur_observing">Amateur observing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andromeda_Galaxy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Amateur observing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Andromeda Galaxy is bright enough to be seen with the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a>, even with some light pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[105]</a></sup> Andromeda is best seen during <a href="/wiki/Autumn" title="Autumn">autumn</a> nights in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>, when from mid-latitudes the galaxy reaches <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a> (its highest point at midnight) so can be seen almost all night. From the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>, it is most visible at the same months, that is in <a href="/wiki/Spring_(season)" title="Spring (season)">spring</a>, and away from our equator does not reach a high altitude over the northern horizon, making it difficult to observe. <a href="/wiki/Binoculars" title="Binoculars">Binoculars</a> can reveal some larger structures and its two brightest <a href="/wiki/Satellite_galaxy" title="Satellite galaxy">satellite galaxies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Messier_32" title="Messier 32">M32</a> and <a href="/wiki/Messier_110" title="Messier 110">M110</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Amateur_astronomy" title="Amateur astronomy">amateur</a> <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a> can reveal Andromeda\'s disk, some of its brightest globular clusters, dark dust lanes and the large star cloud <a href="/wiki/NGC_206" title="NGC 206">NGC 206</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[108]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Andromeda_Galaxy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portal-puzzle.svg"><img alt="Portal-puzzle.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="28" data-file-width="32" decoding="async" height="14"
